[1] Prologue
[2] Joseph's Beard
[3] The beard was peculiar, strange, even a bit comical, shaped like a French spade, slightly curled, hanging down from the chin, hiding the edges of the collar. The perfect little curls of the beard had a feminine touch, appearing noble, like the splendid beard of Zeus. But what was most captivating was not the wavy rhythm of the long beard; what was truly breathtaking was the color of the beard.
[4] This was undeniably Joseph's (1) beard, multicolored, with stripes crisscrossing each other, resembling his robe, shimmering with surprising black, blue, and green. Was the beard colored by the mischievous sun? Or did the wearer of the beard have a hidden agenda, removing the long beard and placing it on the table in the laboratory, dyeing it with a pot of chemicals? This beard that only a god could own, might have an equally astonishing origin. It seemed to be a historically significant beard that should belong to a museum, preserved for future generations to admire.
[5] Sam, a retired New York police lieutenant, now runs a private detective agency to soothe his restless spirit. After forty years of police work, he has developed an immunity to human astonishment. But this time, he was startled and then fascinated: on a warm Monday morning in May, the visitor's beard was truly extraordinary and provoked many thoughts. In his past experiences, the lieutenant had never seen such a combination of brilliant colors. He kept opening his eyes wide, as if he could never get enough of it.
[6] He finally said, "Please sit down." His voice was weak, and he glanced at the calendar, wondering if he had been bewitched and forgotten it was April Fool's Day. Then, leaning back in his chair, he scratched his stubbly chin, looking at the visitor with a mixture of surprise and awe.
[7] The Rainbow Beard sat down nonchalantly.
[8] Sam the lieutenant scrutinized him, noting that he was tall and thin, wrapped up like a mummy, as mysterious as his chin. He was dressed in many layers of clothing, looking like his body was wrapped in layers of thick cloth. The lieutenant's trained eyes caught a glimpse of the man's thin wrist above the gloves, and his slender legs – evidence that he was indeed a thin man. Blue glasses obscured his eyes. He appeared indifferent; the bizarre hat he wore did not come off as he entered the lieutenant's office, hiding his head shape and hair color.
[9] He fell into a gloomy silence, reminiscent of Zeus.
[10] Sam coughed and encouraged, "What can I do for you?"
[11] The man with the mustache moved, seeming to perk up.
[12] "Ah, what can I do for you?" He asked.
[13] His two thin legs suddenly crossed, and his gloved hands clasped together on his knees. "I guess you are Inspector Sam, right?" The man's voice was slightly hoarse. Sam shuddered nervously, feeling as if he were listening to a god speak.
[14] "I am," the inspector said softly. "You -"
[15] A hand waved, "It doesn't matter, Inspector. The fact is - how should I put it? - I have an unusual request."
[16] The inspector thought, You don't say! – and, shaking off his surprise, rose to his feet. His hand moved lightly to the small switch behind his desk, and a barely audible sound was heard, the gentleman with the multicolored mustache apparently not noticing.
[17] "Usually the person sitting in that chair has a request," the inspector said lightly.
[18] The tip of the man's tongue showed from the mustache around his lips, as if startled by the strange color of the mustache, and quickly withdrew. "Inspector, I can say this: I've been looking for you for a long time. The reason I'm attracted to you – is that you seem to be not a typical private detective."
[19] "Customer satisfaction is our policy."
[20] "Yes, that's right... um – are you really a private detective? I mean, Inspector, you're not connected to the police now, are you?" – the inspector glared at him – "You have to understand, I have to make sure that our transaction remains confidential."
[21] "I'm very tight-lipped," Sam said, looking displeased, "not even in front of my best friends - you're worried about that, right! Brother, I'm not the kind of person who would betray a friend, unless it's something unspeakable. Sam Detective Agency doesn't mix with bad guys."
[22] "No, no, not that kind of thing," the man with the rainbow-colored beard quickly chimed in, "I assure you it's not that kind of thing. It's just that - well, to put it simply, sergeant."
[23] "If it's about your wife and her boyfriend," the sergeant glanced at him, "I'm not interested. We don't run that kind of detective agency."
[24] "No, no, sergeant, it's not a family dispute, it has nothing to do with that. It's - uh, simply said," the man with the rainbow-colored beard said, his breath blowing the colorful beard on his chin, "I want you to keep something for me."
[25] "Oh," Sam changed his tone, "what are you keeping?"
[26] "An envelope."
[27] "An envelope?" the detective said impatiently, "What's in it?"
[28] Rainbow Beard showed unexpected firmness, his lips sealed tight. "No," he said, "I can't tell you. Is that okay?"
[29] The detective's gray eyes stared at the unusual guest for a few seconds, unable to see through the blue glasses. "I understand," it was clear the detective didn't, "keeping it safe – what do you mean?"
[30] "Keeping it safe until I get it back. Like a client would."
[31] Sam yawned, "For God's sake, I'm not a banker. Why don't you go to the bank? That's cheaper anyway."
[32] Rainbow Beard spoke cautiously, "Sergeant, I'm afraid you don't understand what I mean. You know that won't work. I have to give it to someone to be kept properly, you know, someone honest."
[33] "Heard. Understood. Well, anonymous friend, let's see the thing. Look, take a look!"
[34] For a moment, the guest did not respond, but when he did, he was quick and agile, as if he had made a deliberate decision after careful thought. He fumbled around under his layers of clothing with his gloved hands and, after a while, pulled out a long, thick leather envelope. Sam's eyes lit up, and he reached out, the envelope being handed to him – reluctantly.
[35] It was an ordinary envelope that could be bought in any stationery store. The front and back were clean and unmarked. The envelope was sealed with the original self-adhesive tape, and the visitor had pasted six cheap white paper strips on the flap, in various shapes, to prevent others from opening it, obviously anticipating human nature's weaknesses.
[36] "Great," said the inspector. "Great, and not tacky, huh." He seemed to casually feel the envelope, but couldn't feel what was inside. He squinted and asked the quiet guest sitting in front of him, "What's inside? You can't expect -"
[37] Rainbow Beard seemed to smile as his beard twisted upward at the corners of his mouth. "Inspector, I like your persistence, I really do. It confirms what I've heard about you, you know, your reputation is excellent. Your cautious attitude -"
[38] "That's right," Sam said impatiently. "But what's inside?"
[39] The man - if he was a man, Sam suddenly had a whimsical thought - leaned forward. "If I tell you -" he said gruffly, "Inspector, if I tell you - you have an envelope in your hand that contains a clue to a secret, a very important secret, I dare not tell the whole truth to anyone in the world!"
[40] Sam the inspector blinked. He should have known. The peculiar guest's beard, glasses, layers of clothing, and strange behavior - well, this was clearly a madman who had escaped from the asylum! Clues, secrets, anyone in the world... this guy was a madman.
[41] "Ah—take it easy," he said. "Brother, you don't need to be so excited." He quickly reached for the small automatic pistol hidden in his armpit gun bag, the lunatic might be armed!
[42] Rainbow Beard let out a loud laugh, startling Sam. "You think I'm crazy. Sergeant, it's not your fault. I can understand why my words might sound—sound—ridiculous. But I swear to you," the strange, hoarse voice became clear and steady, "what I'm telling you is the truth, without exaggeration. Sergeant, you don't need to draw your gun, I won't bite you."
[43] Sam withdrew his hand from his coat, flushing red and glaring at the guest.
[44] The guest continued triumphantly, "That's better, really. Now listen carefully, because I don't have time, it's crucial that you understand. I repeat, the envelope contains a clue, Sergeant, a clue to a colossal secret. I'll add one more thing," his tone grew serious, "a secret worth millions!"
[45] "Hmph, if you're not crazy, then I must be," Sam said grumpily. "You want me to believe your nonsense, you need to tell me more. A secret worth millions—what do you mean? In this flat envelope?"
[46] "Undoubtedly."
[47] "Political secret?"
[48] "No."
[49] "Oil mine? Extortion - love letters? Treasure? Jewels? Come on, brother, be clear. If I don't know what's going on, I won't bother with this."
[50] "But I can't tell you." Rainbow Beard answered with a hint of impatience, "Don't be silly, Chief. I swear on my honor that the contents of the envelope are not evil. This secret is quite legitimate and has nothing to do with the mundane things you mentioned earlier; it's much more interesting and valuable. Remember, the envelope contains a clue to the secret, not the secret itself. I'll say it again, the envelope contains a clue to solving the secret."
[51] Sam exclaimed angrily, "You're driving me crazy. Why the secrecy? Why should I keep this damn thing?"
[52] "There's a good reason for it." Rainbow Beard pursed his lips, "I'm following a trail - the 'source' of the clue, the secret I mentioned. You know, I haven't found it yet. But this clue is very important, really important! I believe we can succeed. Alright, if - if something happens to me, Inspector, I want you to open this envelope."
[53] "Huh." The Inspector grunted.
[54] "If something happens to me - when you open the envelope - you'll see my little clue. It can guide you in a roundabout way to - me, or rather, my fate. Understand, I'm not asking for someone to avenge me. If something happens to me, I'm not interested in revenge; I just want to keep the original secret intact. Is that clear enough?"
[55] "Damn it!"
[56] The man with the rainbow beard sighed. "The clue in the envelope is just a clue, nothing more. The clue itself doesn't reveal anything, but that's exactly what I want! This incomplete trait can protect me from—dear Inspector, I mean no offense—your curiosity, or anyone else's curiosity who might open the envelope. If you open it before I ask you to, I assure you there's nothing meaningful in it for you."
[57] "Oh, come on!" The inspector stood up, his face turning purple. "You're trying to play games with me, you think you're scaring someone with this kid's trick? Get out of here. I can't waste—"
[58] There was a persistent noise on the inspector's desk, but the guest ignored it. The inspector suppressed his imminent anger and picked up the handset of the intercom phone. A female voice bombarded his ear. He reluctantly listened for a while, then put the handset back and sat down.
[59] "Go on," he said grumpily. "Continue. Give me the thing, I'll bite it, I'll swallow the hook, the line, and the sinker. Anything else?"
[60] "Oh, my God, my God," the man with the rainbow beard said with concern. "Inspector, really, I didn't mean to—that's all."
[61] "Nothing to say, right?" the officer said gloomily, "If I'm going to bite, I'll do it properly. There must be something else. This is crazy, but the craziest thing is what you're saying."
[62] The visitor stroked his distinctive beard and murmured, "I'm starting to like you more. Yes, not just these things. You must promise not to open this envelope unless -"
[63] "Unless what?" Sam growled.
[64] The visitor licked his lips. "It's May 6th today, two weeks from now, on the 20th, I'll call you. I believe I'll call you on that day. Then on June 20th, July 20th - every 20th of the month until I find it, I'll call you. I'll call you by the day, and you'll know I'm still alive and haven't met any unexpected danger. If that's the case, just put the envelope in your safe and wait for me to come back. If things don't go as planned, and I don't call you by midnight on the 20th, you'll know I may not be able to call at all. Then - only then - you can open the envelope and see what's inside. Do as you please, I trust your judgment won't be wrong."
[65] Sam sat there, black-faced and pouting, utterly exasperated, his nose twitching with a hint of mockery. "You're really going to great lengths to protect your secret, aren't you? Someone's after you, right? You think someone will kick you out and take it from you before or after you, right?"
[66] "No, no," the rainbow-bearded man exclaimed, "you're mistaken. As far as I know, no one is after this - this secret. But it's always possible that someone might want to know, and I don't know their motives or identity. I'm just being cautious about the unlikely scenario. It's almost impossible, so I won't tell you my name or anything else! Because if nothing happens - I can't think of what could happen - I don't want anyone else to have a clear lead to inquire about my secret. I believe that's clear enough, sergeant..."
[67] "Good God," the sergeant grumbled, "is that too much? Listen, you're mistaken. I thought you were crazy at first, and now I don't know what to think, but listen: if you leave this place immediately, I'll feel better. Go! Out!"
[68] The visitor's confusion was evident on his face. The phone rang again. Sam jumped up, his face flushed with embarrassment, as if a boy caught stealing an apple, his fists stuffed into his pockets. "Alright, alright." He stammered into the phone and then loudly put it down, "I'm sorry. I - I woke up in a bad mood this morning. I don't think I'm used to this kind of -"
[69] The visitor breathed a sigh of relief. "I'm so glad - ah. Very clever, sergeant, really very clever. I hadn't thought of that. " He chuckled, rubbing his hands together, "Really, it's quite exhilarating. Like the mad adventures of Robin Hood."
[70] "Whose?" Sam asked curiously.
[71] "Notorious Arsenè Lupin (2). Ah, a code! A code, of course! When I called you, I should have said—let me think—Ha! 'I'm from outer space. A million!' I said, and you would have known it was me. Ha, ha!"
[72] "Ha, ha," the detective said. "‘I'm from—'” He cautiously shook his head, and a glimmer of hope shone in his eyes. "But, my fee—”
[73] "Ah, your fee," the man with the rainbow-colored mustache said. "Yes, yes, I almost forgot. Detective, what is your fee for taking on this strange little case?"
[74] "Just for putting that annoying envelope in my safe?"
[75] "That's right."
[76] "That would cost you -" The sergeant said urgently, "Just five hundred jingles."
[77] "Jingles?" Rainbow Beard repeated, obviously confused.
[78] "Iron Man, dollars, money! Five hundred dollars!" Sam shouted. He eagerly searched the customer's face for signs of difficulty: the chin that should have dropped under the terrible beard should have dropped, he hoped, so that the outrageous demand would scare off the visitor and he could breathe a sigh of relief without losing face.
[79] "Oh." The client smiled slightly, not at all surprised. He reached into his loose-fitting clothes and pulled out a thick wallet, withdrawing a crisp bill and dropping it onto the table.
[80] It was a brand new thousand-dollar bill.
[81] "I think," Rainbow Beard said lightly, "that a thousand yuan is a reasonable price. This is an unusual, and – ah – unconventional case, and besides, it's worth it for me. It brings a sense of security –"
[82] "Um – uh." Sam swallowed and touched the bill with his fingers, astonished.
[83] "It's settled then," the client said, getting up. "There are two more conditions, officer. I require you to strictly adhere to them. First, you cannot – how did you put it? – have someone follow me when I leave the office; and second, you cannot contact me unless I haven't called you on a certain twenty-day day."
[84] "Of course, of course." Sam said in a trembling voice. A thousand yuan! Tears of joy welled up in his flinty eyes. These days business had been so slow! To lock a flat envelope in the safe and receive a thousand yuan!
[85] "Second –" the visitor said lightly, heading towards the door, "if I haven't called you on a certain twenty-day day, you cannot open the envelope – unless in the presence of Mr. Philo Vance."
[86] The inspector's mouth gaped like a bat hole, and this last blow was a knockout. The Rainbow Beard sneered and strode out, disappearing.
[87] Perkins Sam, female, over twenty-one years old, straightforward, fair-skinned, with golden hair. From a gardening perspective, she was her father's apple, the saying goes, the apple of his eye. She quickly pulled off her headphones and neatly placed them in the bottom drawer of her desk in the front hall. This drawer was used to receive the conversations from the wiretaps installed in her father's modern office. The door opened, and the tall, blue-eyed, blue-bearded stranger appeared, seemingly unaware of Perkins. He seemed to have only one purpose: to quickly remove his glasses, beard, and himself from Sam Detective Agency. The door slammed shut behind him, and at that moment, Perkins - lacking the refinement to make any promises - rushed to the door, catching a glimpse of the magnificent beard sweeping around the corner of the corridor. The owner of the beard looked down on the elevator and ran downstairs. Perkins licked her lower lip, wasting precious three seconds, then shook her head - refinement prevailed, and she had to rush back to the front hall. She rushed into her father's room, her blue eyes shining with excitement.
[88] Sam Inspector remained stunned, sitting powerlessly at the desk, one hand holding a leather envelope and the other holding a thousand-yuan bill.
[89] "Patty (3)," he croaked, "Patty, did you see it? Did you hear it? That guy is weird, isn't he? Am I crazy or is he crazy? What's going on?"
[90] "Oh, Dad," she exclaimed, snatching the envelope and looking up and down; her fingers patted and pressed, and something rustled inside. "Hmm, there's another envelope inside, a different shape. It seems to be square, dear dad, I think -"
[91] "Hmm, you don't think so," the officer hastily snatched the envelope, "remember, I took this guy's money. Petty, that's ten one-hundred, a thousand bucks!"
[92] "You're so mean," Perkins complained, "I don't understand why -"
[93] "Listen, kid, that means you get a new dress, that's all there is to it."
[94] The officer stuffed the envelope into the most secret corner of the office safe, then closed the iron door and sat back down at the desk, wiping the sweat from his eyebrows.
[95] "I should have kicked him out," he grumbled, "I've never seen anything so crazy. If you hadn't called me, I would have kicked him out. Crazy. If anyone wrote this conversation down in a book, no one would believe it was true."
[96] Petty's eyes were dreamy. "It's a cute case, really cute!"
[97] "For a crazy person." The officer gritted his teeth, "I would have thrown him out if it wasn't for the thousand-dollar bills..."
[98] "It's not!" Petty suddenly said. "He...he's an oddball. I don't think he's crazy. Dad, he's not insane! — It's impossible for someone to be able to dress up like a character from a fairy tale, and...I think you were impressed by his beard too, right?"
[99] "Beard! It looks more like dyed wool."
[100] "It's a work of art, a strange work of art. Those curly hairs are so interesting, but...I can tell this guy needs to disguise himself—" Petty murmured, "I can see that this person needs to disguise himself—"
[101] "You noticed that too?" the sergeant said grimly. "It's the strangest disguise I've ever seen. The beard, the glasses, all those clothes—they're all to hide his true appearance."
[102] "No doubt about it," Perkins said. "But why dye his beard colorful? Dad, what do you think?"
[103] "Tell you, he's crazy. Green and blue beard!"
[104] "Maybe he's trying to convey something?" Perkins sighed. "But that's ridiculous! If we remove his disguise, he should be a tall, lean man with distinct features, probably middle-aged, with a slight nasal voice—"
[105] "His voice is also disguised," the sergeant muttered. "You're right, it has a slight nasal quality. But Perkins, he's not from Maine. His nasal quality isn't that of a Maine accent."
[106] "Of course not. You must have noticed, Dad, he's British."
[107] The officer slapped his thigh. "Well, Perkins, you're right! He couldn't hide that."
[108] "He couldn't hide that either," Perkins frowned, "and also, some of his words are British. His accent is Oxford, not Cambridge. Later, he couldn't understand all your colloquialisms, which might have been intentional. He seems to be a cultured person, doesn't he?"
[109] "He has a sly air about him," Sam said grumpily. He took a cigar and put it in his mouth, glaring at his daughter. "But there's one thing he said that bothered me. If he doesn't call by the 20th, we have to open the envelope, but we have to get old Poirot to open it. Why?"
[110] "Yes, why?" Perkins repeated curiously, "Out of all the things about this man's visit, that's the most peculiar thing."
[111] They sat quietly, looking at each other thoughtfully. The unusual request made by the disguised British man before leaving made other mysteries pale in comparison. Mr. Hercule Poirot, although a colorful character, was also the world's most un-mysterious old gentleman. He was in his seventies, retired from the stage more than twelve years ago, living in a spacious manor in Westchester, modeled after Elizabethan England. He led a regular life as a wealthy retired artist. He called his manor Hamlet's Castle, which suited him well. He was one of the greatest actors to portray Shakespeare's plays in his time. At the age of sixty, his career was at its peak, unrivaled, but he suddenly became deaf. Thanks to his superhuman intelligence, he was able to see through things that others couldn't. He decided to learn lip reading – he eventually became an expert in it – and retired to Hamlet's Castle to live off his substantial wealth, providing shelter for struggling colleagues and impoverished artists. Hamlet's Castle became a sanctuary for learning, and its theater became a laboratory for experimental drama. The Elizabethan artifacts in its possession became a Mecca for Shakespeare scholars eager to learn. Out of pure interest, the elderly gentleman in the drama world turned his undeniable talent towards solving crimes. It was then that he met Sergeant Sam, who was still working in the New York Police Department's Detective Bureau at the time, and the two developed an unusual friendship. After Sam retired and established his own private detective agency, the two collaborated on many murder cases, with remarkable results. Later, Sam's daughter Percy joined them, having studied abroad in Europe with a tutor during her teenage years and then returning to her birthplace. She immediately threw herself into the work, working alongside her father and the veteran actor on practical matters.
[112] Sam and his daughter were perplexed. This somewhat uncouth mysterious guest, claiming to have a million-dollar secret, had what in common with their deaf, ailing, upright, beloved, and talented old friend, Mr. Poirot?
[113] "Should I write to him?" Percy murmured.
[114] The sergeant threw away his cigar in disgust. "Petty, don't. Tell you, this whole thing is a mess. Mr. Poirot and our relationship is well-known, and this man with the fake mustache is probably just trying to scare us with the name of Mr. Poirot. He's playing tricks! There's no need to bother Mr. Poirot. We can wait until the twentieth. The man with the big beard won't call on the twentieth – he doesn't want to. He wants us to open the envelope. Everything is set up, and I don't even know about it. That's not a good feeling. ... We're not in a hurry to let Mr. Poirot handle this matter."
[115] "Do as you say." Percy said obediently, but when her gaze fell on the locked iron door of the safe, a deep furrow appeared between her eyebrows.
[116] The situation was that the Inspector had completely misjudged it. At noon on May 20th, Sam's phone rang, and a slightly hoarse British accent said, "Inspector Sam? I'm from outer space. A million!"
[117] "That's right."
[118] Perkins, who was listening to the phone on the extension, felt her heart race.
[119] "It's a million!" The hoarse British accent said. Then, a giggle was heard on the other end of the line, and the Inspector had not yet recovered from his surprise when the phone hung up with a click.

[122](2) Arsène Lupin, a fictional character from the French writer Maurice Leblanc's stories, is a gentleman thief.
[123](3) Pat, nickname of Patience.
[124] Chapter One
[125] Blue Hats
[126] On May 28, Tuesday, around 10:00, Perry Sam, who had flexible working hours, walked into the Sam Detective Agency's lobby, smiling cheerfully at the gloomy Miss Broadie, the agency's stenographer, and then walked into the inner room to find his father listening intently to a serious and earnest guest.
[127] "Ah, Pat," said the Inspector, "I'm glad you came so early. This is Mr. George Fisher, who has an interesting little story. Fisher, my daughter, is a bit like her father's nanny. " He chuckled, "She's the brains of this place, so you might as well tell her everything."
[128] The guest pushed his chair back and awkwardly stood up, fidgeting with his hat. It was a soft-brimmed cap with a small enamel badge on the brim that read "Rivoli Bus Company." He was a tall, muscular young man with an attractive appearance, bright red hair, and a neat blue-gray uniform that fit snugly on his bulky frame. He wore a black belt diagonally across his chest, which was connected to a wide belt around his waist, and his sturdy legs were tightly wrapped in boots.
[129] "Nice to meet you, Miss Sam," he murmured, "It's not a big case—"
[130] "Mr. Fisher, please sit down," said Perry Sam with a smile, the kind she reserved for young and handsome clients. "What's the trouble?"
[131] "Oh, I just told the chief a bit about it," Fischer said, his ears turning red. "I don't know if it's serious. Maybe it is!" He glanced at Donohue, who was his friend, you see, but -"
[132] "Wait a minute!" the chief interjected. "Fischer, I think it's best to start from the beginning. Percy, Fischer drives a large sightseeing bus for Rivoli Bus Company, which is parked near Times Square. He's worried about a friend of his, and he came to see us because of this friend. This guy Donohue is always bringing up my name. Donohue used to be a cop, and I remember he was a big, strong guy with a good service record."
[133] "Does Donohue work for your company?" Percy asked, sighing at the dull beginning of the story.
[134] "No, Miss. He retired from the police force about five years ago and took on a job as a special guard at the British Museum. It's at the intersection of Fifth and Sixty-fifth Streets - the British Museum."
[135] Percy nodded. The British Museum was a small but respected institution dedicated to the display of ancient British manuscripts and books. She had accompanied Mr. Lane there a few times, as he was also a patron of the museum.
[136] "Donoaho used to hang out with my father, and I've known him since I was a child."
[137] "Did he have an accident?" Perns asked.
[138] Fisher fidgeted with his hat. "He... Miss, he's missing!"
[139] "Ah!" Perns said, "Dad, you're better suited for this job. An honest, respected, middle-aged man going missing is usually for a woman, isn't it?"
[140] "Oh, no, Miss," the bus driver said. "Donoaho wouldn't do that."
[141] "Did you notify the missing persons department?"
[142] "Miss, no. I… I don't know if I should. If I start making a fuss, old Donahue would be upset. You understand, Miss Sam."
[143] "Indeed, strange," said the Sergeant. "Percy, there's something fishy about this. Fisher, continue, tell Miss Sam what you told me."
[144] Fisher told a strange tale. A group of teachers from Indianapolis came to New York for a holiday and study trip, renting a large bus from Rivaldo Bus Company for their tour of the city. They had previously arranged the route in a letter. Fisher was assigned to drive them. Yesterday, on Monday, they boarded the bus at the starting point of the company at 44th Street on Broadway at exactly noon. Their final destination was the British Museum. The museum was not usually part of the company's tour route, and the reason was simple: it was "a place for bookworms," as Fisher bluntly put it. Most tourists preferred to see Chinatown, the Empire State Building, the Metropolitan Museum of Art - only the classical exterior - Radio City, the East Side, and Grant's tomb. However, a group of schoolteachers was not an ordinary tourist group; they were art and English teachers in the interior, whom Fisher referred to as "a bunch of bookworms" in a disrespectful working-class tone. Visiting the famous British Museum had become a must-do for art enthusiasts visiting New York. Initially, they were disappointed to return empty-handed because the museum had been closed for several weeks for extensive renovation and interior decoration, with plans to remain closed to the public for at least the next two months. However, the director and board of the British Museum granted special permission for this group of visitors, who could only stay in New York for a short time, to enter the museum.
[145] "Miss Sam, that's where things get strange," Fisher's pace slowed down. "When they got on the bus, I counted the people - I didn't have to do it, because the person in charge of such special guests would arrange everything, and I just had to drive; but I guess I got into the habit, so I counted. There were nineteen people. Nineteen men and women…"
[146] "How many men and women were there?" asked Percy, his eyes brightening.
[147] "I'm not sure, Miss. We left the depot with nineteen people. And you know what happened?"
[148] Percy laughed. "My mind is completely blank. Mr. Fisher, what do you think?"
[149] "I've thought a lot about it." said the bus driver. "We returned to the depot after half the afternoon - the company's rule was always to start and end the tour at 44th Street - Miss, we returned there, and the passengers began to leave, and I counted again, God, there were only eighteen people!"
[150] "I see." said Percy. "It is indeed strange. But what does this have to do with your friend Donohue's disappearance?"
[151] "My friend Donohue, " the inspector said slowly, "came into the picture later. You see, the story is getting more complicated. Fisher, continue."
[152] "Who disappeared?" Pechinsky asked. "Didn't you check with the group?"
[153] "Miss, I didn't have time. Later, I realized who wasn't with us. I noticed him during the tour because he seemed strange. He was probably a middle-aged man with thick gray sideburns – the kind you see in the movies. He was thin and tall. He wore a strange hat – something like blue – and he wore it all day. Now I remember – he didn't talk to anyone else. And then he disappeared – he didn't come back with us."
[154] "Hmm, strange," the inspector said.
[155] "Very strange," Pechinsky said. "Mr. Fisher, what about Donohue? I still don't see the connection."
[156] "Well, ma'am, when we arrived in Britain, I handed the passengers over to Dr. Jotter –"
[157] "Ah, Dr. Jotter," Percy said cheerfully. "I've met the museum's curator."
[158] "Yes, ma'am. He took them away, showed them the exhibits. My work was done until we returned, so I went to the door to chat with Donohue. I hadn't seen him for a couple of weeks, and we had arranged to meet last night at Madison Square to watch a fight –"
[159] "A fight, Mr. Fisher?"
[160] Fisher looked puzzled. "Yes, ma'am, a fight, a boxing match at the square! I'm pretty good with gloves myself, I like quick punches... well, I told Donohue I'd meet him after dinner. He lives in a rented apartment in downtown Chelsea. Then I followed the passengers around, and when they were done, I brought them back to the station."
[161] "Was Donohue still at the door when you and the passengers left the museum?" the inspector asked thoughtfully.
[162] "No, Inspector, at least I didn't see him. After work last night, I had a bite to eat—" Mr. Fisher's face reddened, "then went to Donohue's place to find him, but he wasn't there. His landlady said he hadn't come home yet. I thought maybe he had to work late, so I hung around for an hour. I still didn't see Donohue, so I called a couple of his friends. They hadn't spoken to him all night. That's when I started to get worried."
[163] "A big man like you?" Mr. Percy murmured, looking at him intently, "What else?"
[164] Mr. Fisher swallowed nervously. "I called the British Museum and found the night watchman—a man named Burch—he told me that he saw Donohue leave the museum that afternoon, before my passengers, and I was still there; but Donohue never came back. I didn't know what to do, so I went to the boxing match."
[165] "Poor fellow." Mr. Percy sympathized, and Mr. Fisher suddenly became more masculine again, "Is that all?"
[166] Fisher's broad shoulders drooped, and the warrior's air left his eyes. "Miss, that's the whole story. This morning before I came here, I went to his apartment again, but he hadn't come home all night. I called the museum, and they told me he hadn't come to work yet."
[167] Percy continued to ask: "But Mr. Fisher, what's the connection between your friend Donohue's disappearance and the passenger's disappearance? I was a little dull this morning."
[168] Fisher tightened his jaw. "I don't know, but -" he went on stubbornly, "the disappearance of the man in the blue hat happened around the same time as Donohue's. I couldn't help but wonder if there was a connection.” - Percy nodded thoughtfully - "Miss, the reason I came here, as I said -"
[169] Fisher's tone became heavier. "Miss, the reason I came here, as I said -" Fisher's tone became heavier. "Miss, the reason I came here, as I said, is that if I go to the police station, Donohue might not be happy. He doesn't trust others, Miss Saunders; he can handle it himself. But - damn it, I'm worried about him, and I want the sergeant to look into it, just for old times' sake."
[170] "Well, sergeant," Percy said softly, "can you resist the temptation to your vanity?" Her father smiled. "I'm afraid not. We don't charge for this, Fisher, and times are tough, so I think we'll just make some inquiries."
[171] Fisher's childlike face lit up magically, and he exclaimed, "Wonderful! Inspector, you're too kind."
[172] "Well, let's get to work then!" The inspector's tone was light. "Fisher, have you ever seen this man in the blue hat before?"

[174] "How do you know that?" Pechin asked in surprise.
[175] "Oh, when we all walked into the museum, Donohue carefully looked at each of the nineteen passengers, one by one. He didn't tell me he recognized anyone; if he had, he would have told me."
[176] "I'm not quite following you," the sergeant said mildly, "but I think you must be right. Describe Donohue to me, if you would. I can't remember him—it's been about ten years since I last saw him."
[177] "He's big, about 175 pounds, I'd say," Fisher answered quickly, "five feet ten inches tall, sixty years old, as strong as a bull, with an Irishman's red face, a bullet scar on his right cheek—Sergeant, you remember that one; you'd never forget it if you saw it—walks slowly, with a bit of—"
[178] "Pompousness?" Percy used the right word.

[193] Chapter Two
[194] Seventeen Middle and High School Teachers
[195] These intellectuals were all ladies and gentlemen, none under forty, mostly women, with a few shabby-looking men thrown in. They sat around a table filled with delicious breakfast in the main restaurant of the Park Mountain Hotel, chattering like a flock of sparrows who had discovered the first new buds of spring.
[196] It was well past morning, and except for this group of teachers, the restaurant was empty. The headwaiter sneered and pointed his thumb at the group of vacationing ladies and gentlemen. Like a well-trained army, their surprised eyes turned to observe the intruder. Sergeant Sam's appearance was not one that could inspire trust in children or shy adults. His face was large, red, and fierce, with coarse bones and a crooked nose that made people shudder.

So, he became a kind of pirate, printing 'The Passionate Pilgrim,' which collected two sonnets by Shakespeare that were not previously published, and three sonnets from the already published play 'Much Ado About Nothing,' with the rest being filler. He audaciously claimed that all the poems were written by Shakespeare. I have no doubt they sold well. As for Shakespeare himself, as a playwright, he was surprisingly indifferent to publishing matters."

[1727] The Mysterious House

Phone lines and cables stretched mysteriously from the house and the garage into the wild.
[1736] "What a lovely ruin!" Percy exclaimed.
[1737] "Shh!" The sergeant said sternly, "Alright, Dromio. The rest of you stay here. Joe, when I go to scout ahead, don't get any bright ideas. If you cooperate, I promise you won't suffer."
[1738] He quickly climbed out of the car, crossed the open ground, stepped onto the porch, and despite his bulky frame, his movements were surprisingly light. The paint on the door was just as weathered as the wall paint, but it was still a sturdy piece of wood. There was a small bell next to the door. He didn't ring the bell, but tried to look through the windows, only to find that the tightly closed shutters didn't allow him to peek inside. He lightly descended the steps and disappeared to the left of the house. Three minutes later, he reappeared from the right, shaking his head.
[1739] "Damn it, it seems like an abandoned house. Alright, let's take a look!" He strode confidently onto the porch and pressed the doorbell with force.
[1740] For a moment - so quickly that it seemed he must have been peering through a crack - a man opened the door and stepped out. As soon as the door opened, a bell rang above it. The old-fashioned device hung above the door, and as soon as the door moved slightly, the spring attached to the bell would vibrate and jump. The man was tall and old, with a pale face covered in wrinkles. His cloudy gray eyes briefly rested on the sergeant, then looked at the sunlit carriage, and then back at him.
[1741] "Sir, is there something you need?" His voice was shrill, "Can I be of service?"
[1742] "Is this the house of Dr. Aleis?"
[1743] The old man eagerly nodded, looking somewhat pleased. He smiled and scratched his head. "Oh, yes, sir. Do you have news of him? I've been worried –"
[1744] "Ah, I see. Wait here," the sergeant walked to the edge of the porch, "you all better come up. It seems we'll be waiting for a while."
[1745] The emaciated old man led them through the narrow corridor to a small living room. The interior was cool. The furniture in the room was old and shiny, with an old carpet and old paintings. A pungent odor reminiscent of a funeral home filled the air. The old man busily opened the shutters and curtains, and the room seemed even more shabby and repulsive under the sunlight.
[1746] The sergeant coldly said, "First, we want to know who you are?"
[1747] The old man cheerfully smiled, "Sir, my name is Maxwell. I take care of the house for Dr. Alley, doing all sorts of chores — cooking, cleaning, chopping wood, buying things in Tarrytown."
[1748] "Just a housekeeper? You're the only one here?"
[1749] "Yes, sir."
[1750] "You said Dr. Alley isn't home?"
[1751] Maxwell's smile turned to a look of caution. "I thought - you didn't know, did you? I thought you would have news of him, sir."
[1752] Percy sighed, "Damn it! Mr. Lane, you're right, he must have had an accident."
[1753] "Shh, Petty," her father said, "Maxwell, we need to get some information, we have to find your master. When was the last time -"
[1754] Maxwell's cloudy eyes were full of suspicion. "Who are you?"
[1755] The sergeant quickly produced his shiny badge, an old one he had not returned when he retired, which he used to assert his authority. Maxwell surrendered: "Police!"
[1756] "Answer my questions," Sam said sternly. "When was the last time Dr. Allais was at home?"
[1757] "I'm glad you came, sir," Maxwell murmured. "I was worried, didn't know what to do. Dr. Allais is often out, but this is the longest he's been gone."
[1758] "All right, tell me, how long has he been gone?"
[1759] "Let me think. Today is June 22nd. Oh, it's been over three weeks, sir. That was May 27th... Wait, sir, May 27th was a Monday. That's when Dr. Allais left home."
[1760] "The day the museum had its strange incident," Sam said quietly.
[1761] "Didn't I tell you already?" Vera called out.
[1762] Mr. Lane glanced around the living room and looked at Maxwell anxiously. He said slowly, "Why don't you tell us what happened on May 27th, Maxwell? I think this story will be quite interesting."
[1763] "Well, sir, Dr. Aless left early in the morning and didn't come back until very late in the afternoon, almost evening. He—"
[1764] "How did he look?" Rowe asked curiously, "Excited?"
[1765] "Yes, sir! Excited. He's usually a very reserved person who doesn't show any—any emotions... Sir, you understand what I mean."
[1766] "Did he bring anything back?" Roe's eyes lit up.
[1767] "Yes, sir. It looked like a book. But he was holding the same book in the morning, so—"
[1768] "How do you know it was the same book?"
[1769] Maxwell scratched his chin. "Oh, they looked the same."
[1770] The old gentleman said softly, "That's all right. He took the 1606 Jagger out on Monday morning and came back with the 1599 Jagger, leaving the 1606 one there. Well… continue, Maxwell, what happened next?"
[1771] "Next, sir, Dr. Aless told me before he left, 'Maxwell, I don't need you today, you can go home.' So I prepared his dinner and left—walked down the alley to the road, took the bus back to Tarrytown. I live in Tarrytown, where there's family."
[1772] "Just that?" Sam grumbled.
[1773] The man looked dejected. "I—oh, sir, he told me before I left that he'd leave a package on the hallway the next morning, but he said not to mail it and to take it to Tarrytown and have the post office send it away on Tuesday morning. I came back from Tarrytown on Tuesday morning, Dr. Aless was, of course, not there, but the package was, so I took it to Tarrytown and mailed it as he instructed."
[1774] "What kind of package?" Raine asked eagerly.
[1775] Maxwell looked puzzled. "What? Just a package, flat, I guess—"
[1776] "Was it a book?"
[1777] "Right! It was shaped like a book, sir. It must have been a book."
[1778] "Let's take it one by one," the inspector said impatiently. "Did Dr. Ales come back alone on Monday night? Did you notice anyone peeking around outside?"
[1779] "Oh, he was alone."
[1780] "Did you see a burly Irishman, middle-aged, with a liver-colored face, wandering around? Did you see him?"
[1781] "No, sir."
[1782] "Strange. Where did that damn old fellow hide himself?"
[1783] "Don't forget, Dad," Percy said. "Maxwell left soon after Alec came back. Maybe Donohue was hiding in the bushes, watching Maxwell leave, and then—"
[1784] "And then what?"
[1785] Percy sighed. "Who knows? I'll give him a biscuit for that."
[1786] "Did you notice the address on the package?" the young Rowe asked.
[1787] "Oh, yes, sir. This gentleman…" Maxwell nodded towards Lane, "mentioned that name? The British Museum. It says New York City, Fifth Avenue and Sixty-fifth Street."
[1788] "Brown paper? Address written in blue ink?"
[1789] "Yes, sir."
[1790] Sam said, "Well, that clears up a lot. No doubt the man in the blue hat was Alais. He stole the book, left the one from 1606, and then had it sent back to 1599 the next day by messenger."
[1791] "He's got nowhere to run," Vera said smugly.
[1792] "Good, good," Lane muttered, his eyebrows knitted, "And, Maxwell, do you remember sending a similar package about two months ago?"
[1793] Maxwell's nerves were frayed by the theft of the book, and he became agitated, saying, "I-I hope I haven't done anything wrong. I don't know... Yes, sir, I did send a similar package, to Mr. Crabbe, I believe, at the Saxon house on Fifth Avenue."
[1794] "Your eyesight's fine, isn't it?" the sergeant said coldly. "Well, Joe, your luck's in. You're off the hook."
[1795] "It's quite surprising," the young Rowe whispered. "It seems everything revolves around Dr. Allis. He's causing trouble in Britain and inciting ruffians to raid the Saxon library. What kind of trick is this book playing on us?"
[1796] Joe Vera shrugged his thin shoulders, his beady eyes gleaming. He noticed the sergeant looking at him and tried to appear relaxed. The sergeant said gently, "Joe, be smart and stay out of this. Alright, Maxwell, how long have you been working for Dr. Ales?"
[1797] Maxwell licked his dry lips. "Oh, about three months. He came to Tarrytown... that was at the end of March... He placed an advertisement in the Tarrytown Times seeking an assistant. I applied and got the job. I know when he arrived because Jim Browning, the real estate agent who rented this house, is a friend of mine. Dr. Ales rented the house, paid six months' rent upfront, no contract, no prying, no character references. Browning said it's the way things are done... so we came here, like that. He... he was always good to me."
[1798] "No prying, huh?" Perkins frowned. "Next thing you know, we'll find out he's the Prince of Suriya, on a secret visit to the United States. Maxwell, does your charming host have many visitors?"
[1799] "Oh, no, miss. No one comes... No, I'm mistaken. There was one person."
[1800] "Oh?" Rine said softly. "When?"
[1801] Maxwell furrowed his brow. "He left a week before he left - I don't remember the exact day. It was a man, but his face was covered, and it was night, so I couldn't see his face. He wouldn't give his name, insisted on seeing Dr. Alles. I told him someone was waiting in the living room to see him, Dr. Alles was very excited, initially refused to come out. He still came out, went to the living room, stayed there for a while. He left, leaving the guest alone in the living room. He told me - I think he was nervous - that I didn't have to stay overnight, so I left. The next morning when I came back, he was gone.”
[1802] "Did Dr. Alles ever mention this person, Maxwell? He never told you about this person?" Rowe asked.
[1803] "Me? Sir,” Maxwell chuckled. "No, sir. Not a word.”
[1804] "So who's this guy now?" the inspector grumbled. "Not this guy, Maxwell?” He squeezed Vella's shoulder with his fat hand.
[1805] Maxwell's eyes widened, and he burst out laughing again. "Oh, no, sir. This gentleman doesn't talk like - like that gentleman! That man spoke like Dr. Alles - I mean, a little like an actor.”
[1806] "Actor!" Mr. J. B. Fletcher's eyes widened, and he chuckled. "That's why you said so. You mean a Britisher, do you?"
[1807] "Britisher? Right, sir," Maxwell exclaimed excitedly. "They're both the same."
[1808] "Strange," Percy said softly. "Who is this guy, then?"
[1809] Rowe's eyebrows furrowed even deeper. "Listen, old chap, did Dr. Allais mention anything about leaving when he asked you to go home at 3:27 p.m.?"
[1810] "Nothing."
[1811] "The next morning you came back and found the package, and Alais had already left, did he leave a note explaining where he went, or something like that?"
[1812] "No. I didn't think much of it, sir, but a few days later, he didn't come back—"
[1813] The elderly gentleman concluded, "Inspector, that's why you couldn't find anything about Alais on the missing persons list of the Grayson squad. If Maxwell had reported Alais' disappearance at the time, you might have caught up with him. What a pity!” He shrugged. "Now it may be too late."
[1814] "Dr. Alais—missing?" Maxwell stammered.
[1815] "Apparently so."
[1816] "What am I to do?” The old man wrung his hands. "This house, and all the furniture."
[1817] "Oh, by the way, Inspector," the officer said. "Furniture. Did Alais have furniture when he rented this house?"
[1818] "No, sir. He bought second-hand goods in Terri Town."
[1819] "That doesn't sound like someone who leaves around hundred-dollar bills," Sam thought. "Obviously, he didn't want to stay long." He looked shrewdly at Maxwell. "What does your master look like? Maybe this time we can get a clear description of him."
[1820] "Well, he's tall and quite thin," Maxwell scratched his chin. "I have a photo of him, sir. I enjoy photography, and one day, when he wasn't looking, I took a photo of him."
[1821] "Great! Photos!" Rowe exclaimed. He had been restless in his chair, suddenly jumping up, "Good man, bring it out! Hurry up!"
[1822] Maxwell shuffled to the back of the house as the group stared at each other. The musty smell seemed to intensify, and Verla's nostrils flared as he lit a cigarette. Lane paced silently, his hands clasped behind his back.
[1823] "Photos," Pershing murmured. "Well, that's just what we need to clear up this mess... we can finally solve this tricky problem."
[1824] The old servant soon returned with a small photo. Sam snatched it and quickly examined it under the light, exclaiming in surprise. Everyone gathered around.
[1825] "Look!" Verla shrieked. "I told you so!"
[1826] The photo showed a tall, thin middle-aged man wearing a poorly fitting suit jacket. The photo was clear, and there was no doubt that the person in the photo was Dr. Hamnett Sedra.
[1827] "That confirms what I said," Verla said, triumphantly puffing on his cigarette.
[1828] "That scoundrel," Roebling snarled, lifting his chin. "He lied! I'm going to give this sneaky, vile villain back the bullet in my arm if I can, if I could just—”
[1829] "All right, all right," Reine said softly. "Gordon, calm down. You forgot that we have no evidence against Sedra?"
[1830] "But Mr. Reine," Percy said, "you can't say that this photo isn't evidence!"
[1831] "There's just one thing to do," the sergeant grumbled. "Cuff him and make him tell the truth."
[1832] "Force a British citizen, sergeant?" the elderly gentleman asked coldly. "Please keep calm, everyone. There are too many things that make us lose our minds. If you still want to hear my opinion, you should take it slow, take it slow."
[1833] "But –"
[1834] "Besides…" Reine continued quietly. "There are still things to do. I think we should thoroughly search this house. We might find something."

[1836](1) From Shakespeare's historical play, "Henry VI."
[1837] Chapter Twenty
[1838] Beards and riddles
[1839] Maxwell led the way, entering the stinking little hall, turning right, then left, and descending a wobbly staircase. The carpet was worn and frayed, obviously leading to the upstairs bedrooms. He descended two stone steps and stopped at a massive oak door. It was closed, and he opened it, standing aside: "Dr. Ales is working here."
[1840] The study was spacious, with oak boards lining the floor and ceiling, followed by rows of bookshelves. However, most of the shelves were empty, with only a few sparse books at the bottom.
[1841] "From the appearance of his study," Rowe commented, "he's using it as a temporary hiding place."
[1842] "Indeed it seems so," Reane agreed.
[1843] The ceiling was low, and an ancient, ugly stained-glass chandelier hung from the center, just above the dilapidated desk. The farthest wall had a fireplace with a mantel made of a single block of oak. The blackened grate had burnt-out wood and ashes. An old quill pen, a pot of ink, a reading magnifying glass, and some miscellaneous items were on the desk.
[1844] The inspector and Percy exclaimed together, slamming their hands on the table.
[1845] "What is it?" Rowe shouted, rushing forward.
[1846] A cigarette ashtray lay on the table—a cracked, ugly piece of colored ceramic, with several pitifully small dolphins supporting a bulbous mermaid. Five pale gray shards lay in the center of the ashtray, two of them concave and bearing burn marks. The shards were surrounded by ash and tobacco.
[1847] "It looks like a cheap clay pipe," Rowe said in confusion. "What's so surprising about that?"
[1848] "Donovan!" the sergeant said, naming a person.
[1849] Percy's blue eyes shone as he exclaimed, "That's the evidence! Gordon, Donovan always uses clay pipes. He must have followed Dr. Aless here from the museum that day. It proves he was here."
[1850] "Maxwell!" the sergeant barked. "You said no ugly Irishmen had come to this house recently. How did the pipe get here?"
[1851] "I don't know, sir. I haven't been in this room since Dr. Aless left. I picked up these fragments when I went to send the parcel out that morning before leaving. I placed them on top of the ash and tobacco in the ashtray."
[1852] Reine sighed. "Did you notice these fragments when Dr. Aless sent you away that evening?"
[1853] "They weren't there when I left. I'm certain."
[1854] "Does Dr. Aless smoke a clay pipe?"
[1855] "Dr. Aless doesn't smoke at all. We found this ashtray in the shed when we arrived." Maxwell blinked. "I don't smoke either." He declared solemnly.
[1856] "I think, Inspector -" the old gentleman was tired, "we can connect the dots to some extent. We know that Dr. Allison sent Maxwell away on the 27th evening, and that he was followed from the city to here and waited in the bushes outside the house. We can confirm that. What happened next is only a matter of speculation."
[1857] "This is interesting." Sam frowned. "Let's check the other rooms."
[1858] They climbed the creaky stairs. The upstairs corridor was narrow and had several doors. They checked each room one by one. Two were empty and covered in spider webs, clearly Maxwell was not a diligent caretaker. One was Maxwell's room, with nothing but an iron bed, an old-fashioned washbasin, a chair, and a five-drawer chest found in the basement of some junk dealer.
[1859] Perkins examined the sheets with a woman's eye and asked sternly, "Did you make this?"
[1860] "Yes, Miss," Maxwell swallowed, "the last time was the morning of the 27th."
[1861] "Really?" Reane murmured, "How could that be? You came back on the morning of the twenty-eighth and found Dr. Alais had left, the package was on the corridor downstairs, and you didn't see any signs of someone sleeping in the bed?"
[1862] "No, sir. That's why I knew Dr. Alais must have left the night before, the night he sent me back to Thirlby. Because on Tuesday morning, I found he hadn't slept in the bed."
[1863] "Why didn't you say that earlier?" Sam scolded, "This is important. Whatever happened on that Monday night, it must have happened before Alais appeared, I mean - before Sedra appeared."
[1864] "All right, Inspector," the elderly gentleman smiled, "let's not be too hard on him. Let's just call the missing guest Dr. Alais for now - Dr. Alais. He has a peculiar name, doesn't he? Don't you think so?"
[1865] Gordon Rowe, who had been rummaging through the wardrobe, straightened up and spoke up assertively, "I do find it peculiar. If this treacherous world has any sense or order, this strange name proves the Inspector right and you wrong."
[1866] "Ah, Gordon," Rene's smile remained odd, "I knew things wouldn't escape your hound-like observational skills."
[1867] "What does that mean?" Percy exclaimed.
[1868] "What?" the inspector barked, turning red with frustration.
[1869] Joe Vera sat down in the chair, looking disgusted, as if bored to tears by these madmen. As for Maxwell, he stood with his mouth half-open, staring at them like an idiot.
[1870] "In fact…" Rowe spoke quickly, "Dr. Aless's name has six very unusual letters. Think about it."
[1871] "Letters?" Pechinse repeated, puzzled. "A-l-e-s... Oh, Gordon, I'm so stupid!"
[1872] "What?" the chief of police echoed. "A-1-e-s..."
[1873] "Not A-l-e-s," Raine said. "It's D-r-a-l-e-s."
[1874] Rowe gave Raine a strange look. "So you figured it out too! Pechinse, didn't you realize that Dr. Ales's name formed a very intriguing word puzzle?"
[1875] Pechinse's eyes widened, and his face paled, as he uttered a name.
[1876] "Exactly. Rearrange the letters in Dr. Ales and you get… Sedlar!"
[1877] "Indeed," the elderly gentleman murmured.
[1878] …
[1879] There was a moment of silence, and then Rowe quietly turned his attention back to the wardrobe.
[1880] "Hey!" Sam the sergeant exclaimed loudly, "Boy, you're not as dumb as you look! Reen, you can't deny it now!"
[1881] "Maybe not," Rayne smiled, "I agree with Gordon. The Dr. Ales anagram is too clever to be a coincidence; it's been planned. But what kind of plan? Where from? What for...?" He shrugged, "Since I started exploring the bizarre states of the human mind, I've learned not to jump to conclusions."
[1882] "I'm ready to make my conclusion," the sergeant said sternly, just as Rowe let out a satisfied growl.
[1883] Rowe crawled out of the wardrobe, muttering to himself, and then quickly turned around, placing his uninjured hand behind his back.
[1884] He grinned, "Guess what I found. That old Ales, what a jerk and a sly one!"
[1885] "Gordon, what did you find?" Percy asked, hurrying over to him.
[1886] He gestured with his bandaged arm for her not to come closer. "Wait, miss, mind your reputation." He suddenly lost his smile, "Mr. Rene, you must be interested in this."
[1887] Before everyone recovered from their shock, Rowe turned and delved into the wardrobe again.
[1888] He pulled out three things in succession - a bizarrely colored blue fedora, a pair of blue glasses, and an exaggerated silver-grey handlebar mustache.
[1889] "Today is my lucky day!" the young man chuckled, "Look at these treasures!"
[1890] "Damn it," the inspector said blankly, watching Rowe's admiring expression with a mix of jealousy and envy.
[1891] "Oh, Gordon."
[1892] Reen took the false mustache, glasses, handlebar mustache, and hat from Rowe's hand and whispered, "I think it should be fine, the mustache and glasses should be the same ones you've seen, right?"
[1893] "Listen," Sam grumbled, "there's no way there could be two of these in the world. Can you imagine a sane person wearing a mustache like this?"
[1894] "Certainly, there could be a special case," Reen smiled. "Maxwell, have you ever seen these things before?"
[1895] The butler was startled by the sight of the mustache and shook his head. "I've never seen the mustache or the glasses, sir."
[1896] The elderly gentleman grumbled, "This hat... Verla, this is the hat that Dr. Alessis wore when you followed him to Britain, isn't it? And these sideburns?"
[1897] "Yes, I told you what this guy was up to, I'm not—"
[1898] "The evidence is clear," Reine said thoughtfully, "Inspector, without a doubt, the person who left this letter for you on May 6th and the one who stole the book from Britain on May 27th is the same person. The evidence is clear—"
[1899] "The evidence is clear," the inspector said with a savage hatred in his tone, "This case is clear. With this evidence, along with Clara and Verla's testimony, and that photo, he has nowhere to run. Tell you, there's no Sedra in this case!"
[1900] "No Sedra? Inspector, you scared me. What do you mean?"
[1901] "But there's a Sedra!" Roe argued, and Percy frowned at his father.
[1902] "I've cracked this tangled case, thank God! It's as simple as eating a pastry," the sergeant said. "The man who showed up at the museum, claiming to be their new curator, Dr. Sedra, is not Sedra at all, but Dr. Alessi. Whether he is or not, I'll bet my hat that Alessi killed Sedra before he could arrive in New York and take up his position, then took his place—pretending to be him, they look alike and are about the same height and build, those British people all look alike—and started a series of schemes. I'll tell you, Dr. Alessi is not only a thief, but a murderer!"
[1903] "For me, the question is: Who is Dr. Alessi?" Roe said.
[1904] "You can easily verify your statement," Rains said with a flicker in his eyes. "Just send a telegram to your friend Trunch at Scotland Yard and ask him to send you a photo of Hamnett Sedra."
[1905] "That's a good idea," Percy said.
[1906] "I'm not so sure—" Reane said.
[1907] The inspector's lower lip protruded as he listened to these words, his face suddenly turning a deep purple. He waved his hands and shouted, "I'm crazy! I get nothing out of this whole thing. I'm quitting! This case has kept me awake for days, who cares?"
[1908] "What am I supposed to do?" Maxwell asked helplessly. "I have some money left from Dr. Allis, but if he doesn't come back—"
[1909] "Forget it, lock up the house and go home," Reane said.
[1910] "I don't think so," Mr. Reane whispered. "No, Inspector, I don't think so. Maxwell, I think you'd better stay here and pretend nothing happened."
[1911] "But, sir——" Maxwell scratched his sagging cheek.
[1912] "If Dr. Allais comes back - and it's not completely impossible - I think the sergeant would be glad to hear the news."
[1913] "Okay, sir." Maxwell sighed.
[1914] "Damn it, I don't want to——" The sergeant cursed.
[1915] "Well, you old tyrant," Rene smiled, "give Maxwell a business card... that's better." He took Sam's arm, "Maxwell, remember to notify Dr. Allais as soon as he comes back!"
[1916] Chapter Twenty-One
[1917] The Perils of Westchester
[1918] Suddenly, the case seemed to come to an abrupt end, as if struck by a plague. The entire week lay dead and still, with nothing happening, no new developments, and most importantly, no one caring about what was happening.
[1919] The sergeant, true to his word, let go of the case. He was fully occupied with the jewelry heist case, which had shaken society and involved a priceless pearl necklace and a socialite attacked on Park Avenue. He rarely appeared in the office, only to check his mail. Apart from Percy's occasional visits, Sam Detective Agency was left in the hands of Miss Broadie, who dealt with it with tearful sighs.
[1920] As for Percy, she suddenly became passionate about learning and frequented the British Museum, where she garnered the admiration of many men. The men were still busy restoring the worn-out building and trying to give it a fresh look. She and Mr. Rowe showed diligence in their study of Shakespeare, but their collaboration did not uncover many secrets of the playwright. Apart from discussing the enigmatic Dr. Siddra and their own affairs, Mr. Rowe's studies made little progress.
[1921] But the most indifferent person seemed to be Mr. Philo Vance. He hid himself in the comfortable Hamlet Castle for nine days, remaining in a monastic silence.
[1922] However, there was a minor incident this week. For example, the Inspector's office received two letters. One was from Dr. Leo Shilling, the Chief Medical Examiner of New York County, who made Manhattan's criminals tremble. From a medical perspective, the letters 3HS wM contained no chemical meaning. Initially, he wanted to divide the symbols into several parts. 3HS could represent three parts of hydrogen and sulfur, but there was no such chemical compound, as a hydrogen atom has stubbornly refused to bond with a sulfur atom since the beginning of time. As for the lowercase w, there could be different chemical interpretations, Dr. Shilling continued, such as watt being an electrical term and wolframite being a rare mineral. The uppercase M generally represents a metal. If w represented wolframite, then M and w might be related. The medical expert's conclusion was: "In my opinion, the symbol composed of numbers and capital and lowercase letters is pure nonsense and has no scientific significance."
[1923] The second letter was from Group Captain Sheff of the Washington Intelligence Bureau.
[1924] Group Captain Sheff apologized for taking so long to reply to the unusual question from Inspector Sam. He had been very busy and may not have studied the symbol properly, but in his opinion, the symbol "if it is a code, is a fairy tale." If it were a code, he did not believe it could be deciphered. If it had any meaning, it would require each letter to have a preset secret meaning. Experts might spend months trying to find a rule or method for deciphering it, only to end up without success.
[1925] Percy was almost in tears, spending many nights studying the strange symbol in secret. Rowe tried to comfort her, but it was of no help, and his luck was not good either.

I had a duplicate key hanging on my pants—Dr. Allais had the original key—he took it and left, locking the garage door, and left me in the dark. It was so quiet outside, there was no sound... I spent the whole night in the garage, not daring to breathe. The ropes hurt, and I couldn't sleep. I was so tense, my arms and legs felt numb. But in the morning, I managed to loosen the ropes and take the rag out of my mouth, and found Sam's business card in my pocket. So I called the divisional phone... I guess I must have fainted. In other words, that hole couldn't have been accidentally left open! If the ax-wielder knew about the rose and the hole, he wouldn't have gone around chopping everything up. If it's not the ax-wielder, then it must be someone else. So we have two people, don't we? Let's see what happens!"

Dr. Ales sent Vera to steal the 1599 Jagger from the Saxon family. It was Dr. Ales who sat on the bus rented by the teachers and broke the British Jagger bookcase - Vera's testimony proved this, and the blue cap and gray false mustache found in Dr. Ales' bedroom could further confirm it. But who is Dr. Ales? Is he Hamnet Sedley, as Clara and Vera identified, or is he someone else entirely? Is there any confusion about his identity?"
[2050] "Two: Who is this Hamnet Sedley? According to Scotland Yard, there is indeed a Hamnet Sedley who was hired as the new curator of the British Museum. But is the person who appeared at the British Museum and claimed to be Sedley really Hamnet Sedley? Or is it, as Dad thought, that someone was pretending to be Hamnet Sedley? He must have something he doesn't want to be known, and he lied about the date he arrived. Did Hamnet Sedley die? Did this person steal his name and take his place? Why did he hide the date of his arrival? What did he do between the actual arrival time and the fabricated arrival time?"
[2051] "Ah!" Young Rowe said, "What a profound thought!"
[2052] Perkins glared at him and continued to read aloud: "（3）If Hamnet Sedley is not Dr. Alessi, what happened to Dr. Alessi? Why did he disappear?"
[2053] "（4）What happened to Donohue?"
[2054] "（5）Who chased me and Gordon and took the envelope?"
[2055] "（6）The axe-wielder is who? He is not Dr. Alessi, but someone else."
[2056] "（Seven） Who followed the axeman and opened the secret cabinet? It could be Dr. Aless himself – he would know where he hid things."
[2057] "Wait a minute, Pechins," said Lane. "How do you know the axeman isn't Dr. Aless? And there were two people in the Aless house last night?"
[2058] "No, there's one more," Pechins frowned. "The most important and puzzling question." She continued, "（Eight） What do these points point to? There's no doubt that Dr. Aless's 'million-dollar secret' is involved. But the million-dollar secret is related to the symbol Dr. Aless left for my father, so the solution to everything depends on the last question: what does the symbol mean?"
[2059] After she finished speaking, she put down the paper and began doodling on the table. The two men were silent for a while. Rowe, who had been idly watching Pechins doodle, stood up from his chair, and Pechins and Lane looked at him curiously.
[2060] "What are you writing?" the young man asked sharply.
[2061] "What?" Percy blinked. "Just the symbol 3HS wM that's been driving us crazy!"
[2062] "Hurrah!" Roe exclaimed, jumping up and his eyes shining. "I know! I know! It's so simple and childish!"
[2063] Jules Reine stood up, walked to the table, and his face was illuminated by the light. He said, "You finally figured it out, Percy. When we sat in your father's office and he opened the Saxon letter to see what was written inside, I saw it and understood it too. Gordon, tell her."
[2064] "I don't understand what you two are talking about," Percy complained.
[2065] "How was I sitting when you wrote the symbol?" Roe asked.
[2066] "Right in front of the table, facing me!"
[2067] "Absolutely right. In other words, I saw this symbol just like Mr. Renet saw it when the commissioner opened the original letter. He saw what I saw - I saw it backwards!"
[2068] Percy gasped. She picked up the paper and turned it over. The symbol now looked like this:
[2069] She slowly repeated, "Wm She." Each letter seemed to have a different taste in her mouth, "This looks like a signature. W-m...William..." - both men looked at her intently - "...William Shakespeare (1)!" She shouted, jumping up from her chair, "William Shakespeare!"
[2070] After a while, Percy sat down on the carpet next to the old gentleman's feet, and his pale, slender fingers played with her hair. Rowley relaxed comfortably in front of them.
[2071] "Ever since then, I've been thinking about this over and over in my mind," Reen explained wearily, "When I analyzed it, it seemed quite clear. Dr. Allès was not imitating Shakespeare's signature; this copy should be the handwriting of the Elizabethan era. He simply wrote it in his own way - perhaps thinking he had written it clearly - the uppercase letters in this unusual Shakespeare signature. Unusual because of the lowercase m and the handwritten e. But why is H capitalized? It could be Allès' peculiar behavior, but it doesn't matter. What matters is that -"
[2072] "It's one of Shakespeare's signatures," Rowley said slowly, "It's quite strange!"
[2073] Reen sighed. "Gordon, you know better than me that there are only six genuine Shakespeare signatures in the world."
[2074] "Strangely enough," the young man said, "one of them is written as Will Shak'P'."
[2075] "Yes! There are also some so-called 'suspicious' signatures, one of which has a spelling like Allès' symbol - a capital W, a lowercase m connected to the top of the W, then a capital S, a lowercase h, and a lowercase e that is also connected."
[2076] "Is it like the old English script of 'ye'?" Percy asked.
[2077] "You got it right. This suspicious signature appears in the Metamorphoses of Ovid (2) by Aldine, which is now housed in the Bodleian Library at Oxford."
[2078] "I saw it when I was in England," Rowe said.
[2079] The old man continued, "I checked with the Bodleian Library, and the book of Ovid is still there. I thought the whole thing might be related to the theft of that book. Of course, that's a ridiculous idea."
[2080] "You mean -" Percy's tone was one of awe, "that all these thefts, doubts, and everything else are related to the discovery of Shakespeare's seventh true signature?"
[2081] "It seems so, doesn't it?" Rowe said with a bitter smile. "I've wasted my youth here. Ha, ha! I've never come across anything as momentous as this while going through Elizabethan records."
[2082] "What else could it be?" Raine whispered. "If the secret is really worth a million, Dr. Allès had reason to believe the signature was genuine. Otherwise, how could it be worth a million? Ha, it's a very interesting question."
[2083] The young man murmured softly, "If it has incalculable historical and literary value, it is priceless in itself."
[2084] "I read somewhere that a completely authentic seventh signature of Shakespeare found at an auction was worth a million pounds. I don't know if it's pounds or dollars! But the signature must have a purpose; it is usually associated with some document."
[2085] "The paper in the book!" Percy exclaimed.
[2086] "Shh, Petty. It's not necessarily so," Rowe pondered. "Six real signatures, of course, are associated with certain documents: one on a legal document where the old man was involved in a lawsuit; one on a contract signed when buying a house around 1612; another when paying for the same house; and the last three on his three wills. But it could also be on the back of a book's endpapers, you know."
[2087] "I doubt it," Raine shook his head. "As Percy has already noticed, the seventh signature might be on a document - a transaction document, a lease - where the document itself has little historical value? Hmm, maybe…"
[2088] "The value can't be small," Rowe objected. "If it's a transaction document or a lease, it could be of great importance, possibly explaining where Shakespeare was at some point - to clarify many things."
[2089] "Yes, yes. I said the value was small from a human perspective. But what if it's on a letter?" Raine leaned forward, his fingers tightening in Percy's hair, making her almost cry out, "Think of the possibilities! A signed letter by the immortal Shakespeare!"
[2090] "I was thinking -" Rowe muttered. "This is too much. Who is it for? What does it say? Autobiographical data. A genuine Shakespeare manuscript."
[2091] "Of course, it's within the realm of possibility," the old gentleman's voice sounded slightly strained, "If the signature appears at the end of the letter, the letter would be more valuable than the signature! No wonder the scholars are constantly scheming. It's like - oh, finding the actual manuscript of Saint Paul's letters!"
[2092] "The document was in the 1599 Jagger," Perkins whispered hoarsely, "Dr. Aless discovered the other two existing 1599 Jagger volumes and couldn't find anything, so he resorted to obtaining the third one, which belonged to the collection of Saxen. And he found it! This - is it possible..."
[2093] "It seems so," Rowe smiled, "He found it, lucky man!"
[2094] "But now it's stolen. Oh, dear, I'd bet it was in Dr. Aless' cabinet."
[2095] "It's quite possible," said Reane. "One more thing. I found out that the stolen and returned third book was actually bought by Samuel Saxen from Sir Humphrey, a collector from England."
[2096] "The same person?" Percy was startled.
[2097] "The same." Raine shrugged, "Sir Humphrey died just a few weeks ago. No, no. Don't be alarmed. His death was natural, no foul play. As usual, God was the executioner. He was eighty-nine and died of pneumonia complications. My contact on the other side told me that the Jager Saxon bought from Sir Humphrey was the one that caused all the trouble, and it had belonged to the Humphrey family since the Elizabethan era. Sir Humphrey was the last surviving member of the family, with no heirs."
[2098] "He couldn't have known the document was hidden in the book's cover," Rowe said, "otherwise he wouldn't have sold it."
[2099] "Of course not. In fact, several generations of the Humphrey family didn't know or suspect the existence of the document."
[2100] Percy asked, "But why was the document hidden in the book cover? Who did it?"
[2101] Rine sighed, "That's a question. I think the document has been hidden there for hundreds of years, probably written for a contemporary, who knows? But the document needed to be hidden, and I believe it has a special value or meaning in itself."
[2102] Old Quincy slipped into the study, his wrinkled face resembling a leaf, with no part of it untouched by wrinkles. He tugged at the owner's sleeve and complained, "Mr. Jury, there's a man named Bowlin, a police officer from Tarrytown."
[2103] Rine frowned, "You old knave! What did you say?"
[2104] "He called. He told me that just an hour ago, at seven o'clock" - the study clock pointed to seven - "Aless's house was mysteriously blown up!"
[2105] --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- --- ---
